  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5e65c6cb-0ac0-4898-b60c-eee62c052e32'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer13651453886178821341"><i>From the back jacket:</i><br/><br/>NORMALLY, WHEN THERE WAS NOTHING TO DO, HE LISTENED TO THE SILENCE.<br/><br/>The Silence was very faint here. The roar of air conditioners and computer fans, the susurration of many voices heard but not decipherable.... This was the office of </span>
  <span id="freeText13651453886178821341" style="display:none"><i>From the back jacket:</i><br /><br />NORMALLY, WHEN THERE WAS NOTHING TO DO, HE LISTENED TO THE SILENCE.<br /><br />The Silence was very faint here. Almost drowned out by the sounds of the mundane world. Did people in this polished building understand how noisy it was? The roar of air conditioners and computer fans, the susurration of many voices heard but not decipherable.... This was the office of the transEarth Institute, an arm of the Black Corporation. The faceless office, all plasterboard and chrome, was dominated by a huge logo, a chesspiece knight. This wasn't Joshua's world. None of it was his world. In fact, when you got right down to it, he didn't have a world; he had all of them.<br /><br />ALL OF THE LONG EARTH.<br /><br /><i>From the inside jacket:</i><br /><br /><b>The possibilites are endless. <i>Just be careful what you wish for....)</i><br /><br />1916: The Western Front.</b> Private Percy Blakeney wakes up. He is lying on fresh spring grass. He can hear birdsong, and the wind in the leaves. Where has the mud, blood and blasted landscape of no-man's-land gone? For that matter, where has Percy gone?<br /><br /><b>2015: Madison, Wisconsin.</b> Police officer Monica Jansson is exploring the burned-out home of a reclusive--some said mad, others allege dangerous--scientist who seems to have vanished. Sifting through the wreckage, Jansson finds a curious gadget: a box containing some rudimentary wiring, a three-way switch, and...a potato. It is the prototype of an invention that will change the way humankind views the world forever.<br /><br />The first novel in an exciting new collaboration between Discworld creator Terry Pratchett and the acclaimed SF writer Stephen Baxter, <i>The Long Earth</i> transports readers to the ends of the earth and far beyond. All it takes is a single step....</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1488009-have-read-the-books-but-still-have-an">Have read the books but still have an issue with iron transportable problem.
Fully understand that Iron/ferrous metal cannot step but how does Sister Agnes get her ancient old Harley to Madison West 5? So much iron in a Harley that it would be a pile of pieces after the step. No tank, frame, axles, crankshaft, gear shafts etc. Just a pile of scrap rubbish.</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_336578526" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/336578526" />
    <a title="Mark Lawrence" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4508542-mark-lawrence"><img alt="Mark Lawrence" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442536170p2/4508542.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/336578526?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Lawrence" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Lawrence" href="/user/show/4508542-mark-lawrence">Mark Lawrence</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer336578526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1680830027722929128">Everyone loves Sir Terry. I love Sir Terry. I love the books & have great respect for the man. This review is simply my opinion of the success of this particular collaboration. I'll be 1st in line for the next T.P book and I'd even give T.P + S.B another go. <br/><br/>From the slew of 4 & 5* reviews already on show I may be out on a limb on this one - so don't listen to me - give it a try.<br/><br/><br/>-------------<br/><br/>Collaboration. It’s a word with an unfortunate aftertaste. Collaborators get a bad rap. Sir Terry Pratche</span>
  <span id="freeText1680830027722929128" style="display:none">Everyone loves Sir Terry. I love Sir Terry. I love the books &amp; have great respect for the man. This review is simply my opinion of the success of this particular collaboration. I'll be 1st in line for the next T.P book and I'd even give T.P + S.B another go. <br /><br />From the slew of 4 &amp; 5* reviews already on show I may be out on a limb on this one - so don't listen to me - give it a try.<br /><br /><br />-------------<br /><br />Collaboration. It’s a word with an unfortunate aftertaste. Collaborators get a bad rap. Sir Terry Pratchett’s collaborations with other notable authors have been limited. In 1990 we saw Good Omens, produced with Neil Gaiman. On the crest of that success Pratchett found another partner in Larry Niven but this effort floundered with Niven producing Rainbow Mars in 1999 as a solo work built upon some of their shared ideas. And now 2012 sees The Long Earth, a collaboration with Stephen Baxter. The accompanying promotion contains a photo of the two grandees locked in combat before a laden bookshelf. A certain degree of dynamic tension does help in collaborative writing, too little and you get compromise writing, too much and it’s never going to get finished. I think here both men were pulling punches and the pages could have benefited from a good blooding!<br /><br />	Collaborations can be port and stilton good, or marmite and custard bad. I suspect it’s impossible for two fine writers like Baxter and Pratchett to serve up anything wholly unpalatable, but the Long Earth feels long, although it is short, and is decidedly blah. Think porridge.<br /><br />	The Long Earth is science fiction. In science fiction the IDEA tends to overshadow the characters and even the story. Here the IDEA has flattened both. Glimpses of story and character may be seen poking out from the margins of the IDEA, vital juices pooling. You can’t see much but you can see enough to know you don’t need an ambulance.<br /><br />	The Terry Pratchett of Discworld fame has not shown up to this party. There’s rarely a smile in The Long Earth, and never a laugh. The idea explored is that Earth sits in one of an infinity of parallel universes and that these become opened up to humanity such that pretty much everyone can step through them like moving from one card to the next in a deck of cards. We get to hear a lot about the impact this has on humanity, both social and economic. Sometimes we hear this from characters who pop up and are not heard of again, giving the book a disjointed feel. Of the hundreds of thousands of Earths open to Joe Public almost all are Earth as it would have been without us. A lot of time is spent detailing minor evolutionary variations in the flora and fauna, to the point where you just don’t care about one more slightly smaller elephant or slightly uglier crocodile. A lot of time is spent hopping from one forest world to the next. It’s an idea that is interesting in a paragraph but becomes increasingly dull over 300 pages.<br /><br />	The writing, line by line, is fine. It lacks trade-mark Pratchett sharpness but it does the job. The story arching over chapters is . . . not gripping. There’s essentially no tension in it. Our heroes (a young man who is very good at moving through the worlds, and an artificial intelligence named Lobsang) are exploring, they don’t have any clear goals stated, nothing is after them, they have no serious problems, they don’t appear worried or even to care that much, and thus as a reader one tends to a similar disposition.<br /><br />	The book ends with a bang, but it’s a rather ineffectual one that is, like the bulk of the book, hard to care about. I’m a fan of Sir Terry’s work and I applaud his willingness to experiment with new styles and new writing partners. I hope he keeps doing it. I cannot though, hand on heart say I enjoyed this book. Not even a little.	 <br /><br /><br />Edit: RIP Sir Terry - you left us many great books to remember you by.<br /><br /><br /><br /> <a href="http://eepurl.com/cimnK1" rel="nofollow noopener">Join my 3-emails-a-year newsletter #prizes</a><br /><br /><br />......</span>
  <a data-text-id="1680830027722929128" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2121823602" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2121823602" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2121823602?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2121823602" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9574935955811862484">Science fiction is full of stories that go backwards and forwards; they swing through the spectrum of time and explore the popular trope in all its possibilities. Pratchett and Baxter, on the other hand, step sideways.<br/><br/>The Long Earth is a seemingly endless series of alternate realities with Earth being the focal point. By using a Stepper, a piece of technology that is powered by a potato (Pratchett at work no doubt), the user can travel along the spectrum of Earths and step foot on thousands of d</span>
  <span id="freeText9574935955811862484" style="display:none">Science fiction is full of stories that go backwards and forwards; they swing through the spectrum of time and explore the popular trope in all its possibilities. Pratchett and Baxter, on the other hand, step sideways.<br><br>The Long Earth is a seemingly endless series of alternate realities with Earth being the focal point. By using a Stepper, a piece of technology that is powered by a potato (Pratchett at work no doubt), the user can travel along the spectrum of Earths and step foot on thousands of different versions of their home world. Needless to say, the scope of such a thing is huge and the possibilities extraordinary. Anything could be out there on one of these Earths. Just imagine an Earth new and fresh, green and full of life, one that hasn’t yet been harvested by man. Sounds beautiful. <br><br><i>"All these parallel Earths are identical on all but the detailed level. Oh, save that they are empty. Well, actually they are full, mainly of forest and swamp. Big, dark, silent forests, deep, clinging, lethal swamps. But empty of people. The Earth is crowded, but the Long Earth is empty." </i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506348987i/24007839._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Did this book just reveal my inner misanthropy? The idea completely sold the story to me. The protagonist, Joshua, a boy who can step without the technology, is rather ordinary. He is hired by a crazy scientist called Labsong who wants protection on an exploration mission. Together they travel to the ends of the The Long Earth to see exactly how far it stretches and to see if it is, indeed, infinite as many scientists theorise. The two men are unassuming and very uninteresting in the beginning. They are not what keep the story going. However, by the end it is very clear that their part in the events will be very vital and their chemistry does develop. <br><br>Instead what drove the story forward was wonder, curiosity and, again, possibility. The Idea behind <i>The Long Earth</i> is vastly imaginative and finding exactly what made it work kept me reading. I was in awe at some of the descriptions of the places that the characters found themselves. The descriptions on the first page (that of the image on the cover) were particularly great. I also found the reasons given for natural phenomena and mythological creatures on the real Earth very clever and quite funny. It’s certainly a well thought out book. <br><br>It evoked a sense of wonder as the two men delved into the unknown, I, too, wanted to know what was at the very ends of it all. As far as the authorship goes, the two writers couldn’t be further apart. This collaboration shouldn’t work; yet, somehow, it does. Pratchett is clearly the idea man, his wisdom and subtle humour is tamed by Baxter’s scientific knowledge. The wiring is informative, though Pratchett’s influence frees Baxter’s mechanical and dry prose from its mundanity. It is snappy in places, explanatory and even has a few laugh out loud moments. <br><br>Many of the criticisms for this book centres on its characters and lack of plot drive. For some readers this was a book that didn’t go anywhere, though for me it was a book that went everywhere. I’m not sure how the rest of the series will compare, but I do think this instalment is rather excellent: this series has so much potential. I hope it doesn’t limit itself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9574935955811862484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_809833028" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/809833028" />
    <a title="Carmen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25353295-carmen"><img alt="Carmen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412617445p2/25353295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/809833028?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carmen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carmen" href="/user/show/25353295-carmen">Carmen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780062068682" href="/book/show/16248074-the-long-earth">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Science-Fiction Fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=he-says">he-says</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=traditionally-published">traditionally-published</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=british-author">british-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=published2012">published2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer809833028" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17756433899145588748"><i>"The next world is the thickness of a thought away."</i><br/><br/>This co-authored book (Terry Pratchett and Stephen Baxter) is a wonderful idea, lovingly executed. The Long Earth refers to the discovery of infinite dimensions all next door to one another.<br/><br/>When a scientist publishes the blueprint for a "stepper" - a device that can allow you to step from Earth to the next dimension (Earth 1) and from there onto the next Earth (Earth 2) and the next - online free for all to use, in order to thwart a greedy corp</span>
  <span id="freeText17756433899145588748" style="display:none"><i>"The next world is the thickness of a thought away."</i><br /><br />This co-authored book (Terry Pratchett and Stephen Baxter) is a wonderful idea, lovingly executed. The Long Earth refers to the discovery of infinite dimensions all next door to one another.<br /><br />When a scientist publishes the blueprint for a "stepper" - a device that can allow you to step from Earth to the next dimension (Earth 1) and from there onto the next Earth (Earth 2) and the next - online free for all to use, in order to thwart a greedy corporation's attempt to capitalize on it... it changes the world.<br /><br />The device, so simple any kid could make it - and powered by a common potato - soon leads to hundreds of thousands if not millions of people "stepping" over into other dimensions to make new lives for themselves.<br /><br />At first, it seems as if these other Earths are uninhabited. But eventually animal life is found, animal life that is wildly different than the ones that evolved on Datum Earth (the Earth we know). <br /><br />When Joshua, an orphaned man who was raised by nuns, is signaled out by a new AI to explore the Infinite Earth, he can't resist the chance to explore hundreds of thousands of dimensions away from Datum Earth. But there are plenty of surprises waiting for him, not all of them friendly...<br />...<br /><br />POSITIVE:<br /><br /> - This book is so innovative and so lovingly rendered you can't help but enjoy it, even a little bit, even against your best efforts. Pratchett and Baxter create wild, imaginative worlds that are so similar and yet so amazingly different than the one we know that our minds are blown. <br /><br />Here's an Earth where the dinosaurs were NOT wiped out. Here's an Earth where elephants developed chameleon-like camouflage powers. Here's a world where the moon never came into existence. <br /><br />Hundreds of thousands of worlds, hundreds of thousands of ways for Pratchett and Baxter to stretch their imagination. Beautiful and exciting, like when you saw Jurassic Park for the first time. With the intricacy and thrills of visceral environment, as in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17934530.Annihilation__Southern_Reach___1_" title="Annihilation (Southern Reach, #1) by Jeff VanderMeer" rel="nofollow noopener">Annihilation</a>. <br /><br />Very creative wildlife and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[other sentient beings which have evolved. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Just super-innovative and pure imagination-candy.<br /><br /> - Baxter is into some ugly stuff. Pratchett's little comedic touches only heighten the creepiness, in my mind. It's a creepy world/concept to begin with, and Pratchett's little quirky threads were icing on the cake for me.<br /><br /> - <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Talking <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> robotic AI cat who catches mice, stores them in her tummy, and releases them into the wild.<br /><br /> - Two lesbian characters (separate, NOT in a couple with each other).<br /><br /> - <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The authors resist making Joshua fall in love with or have sex with any of the females, Sally and Helen. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I'm very thankful for this.<br /><br /> - Interesting concept about how no one can travel with iron. I, of course, immediately thought: Fairies. Of course they can't travel to other dimensions with iron. Fairies hate iron.<br /><br />P&amp;B give a more scientific explanation for why iron won't travel - something about it being the most stable element - but I still believe it has to do with fairies and it IS actually possible that I may be correct. (We'll see...)<br /><br /> - Reminiscent of the novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Machine</a>. I wasn't surprised to learn that Baxter had penned a sequel to that famous book.<br /><br /> - Good philosophical and moral questions are raised.<br /><br /> NEUTRAL:<br /><br /> - As in many recent science-fiction novels, hard plug and sell for NASA and NASA funding.<br /><br /> - As in many science fiction novels, a cat and moreover <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ a talking, AI cat. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /> - Very common "X-Men"-type subplot which involves people who are not <s>mutated</s> able to step being very angry and calling to stop and even kill all <s>mutants</s> steppers. Even though it makes no logical sense since they will be having <s>mutant</s> stepper children and grandchildren and they are a minority at 20% of the population.<br /><br /> - Oh, Joshua is the "Chosen One" with "Special Abilities" and by the way, his mom died when she was 15 giving birth to him and no one knows who his father is and he was raised by nuns. YOU KNOW. Typical hero origin. <br /><br /> - Some kind of message or statement about the U.K. that P&amp;B are making, and I'm pretty sure it's not a flattering one. However, as an American I can not quite understand what they are getting at. Perhaps a British reader would be better equipped to unpack P&amp;B's message here.<br /><br />NEGATIVE: <br /> - As in many science fiction novels penned by older white males, a propensity to show you are "progressive" by having homosexual characters, and by that I mean "lesbians." No gay men anywhere to be found. Because, you know. That would be 'icky.' Still can't believe this shit.<br /><br /> - Also says stuff like the female MC was "never lovelier than when she was blowing her top" and this "she's so pretty when she's angry, let's purposefully piss her off, LOL" shit is just so male-author.<br /><br /> - Vignettes. P&amp;B tell a LOT of little vignettes and short stories throughout the book. Don't get too attached to the characters in the short stories, or expect them to show up ever again. They are mainly dropped in here and there to prove points.<br /><br /> - No follow-through. There are a lot of insidious, suspicious avenues that P&amp;B open that are not resolved and just kind of fizzle out. Right off the top of my head, I would say <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the instability and possible evilness of Lobsang, the unsettled and disturbed feeling Joshua gets from Happy Landings, and the growing evilness of Mr. Henry. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /> - Not very good characterization. I'm especially thinking of Sally <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ above all, her apparent envy of Joshua that sprung out of nowhere at the end, leaving my shaking my head in confusion <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and the whole subplot regarding Rod Green and his <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ anger and subsequent terrorist acts as a result of his family's abandonment. I also needed some more insight into how and why his family so easily abandoned him completely and totally; and also more insight into how exactly that led him to terrorism. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Character development is not a high point in this novel. If you are looking for good emotional explaining and psychological insights into the characters' thoughts and actions... you will be very disappointed.<br /><br />Tl;dr - Bright, imaginative, and full of promise: the book has four sequels, the last one expected to be published this year. However, this IS scattered with sci-fi tropes, and I should warn you that characterization and plotline follow-through is hazy and sometimes non-existent. I will definitely be reading the next books. Not as good as books like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17934530.Annihilation__Southern_Reach___1_" title="Annihilation (Southern Reach, #1) by Jeff VanderMeer" rel="nofollow noopener">Annihilation</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13642710.Nexus" title="Nexus by Ramez Naam" rel="nofollow noopener">Nexus</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13033939.vN__The_Machine_Dynasty___1_" title="vN (The Machine Dynasty, #1) by Madeline Ashby" rel="nofollow noopener">vN</a>, but definitely satisfying and stunning in its own way.<br /><br />R.I.P. Terry Pratchett. <i>Earths, untold Earths. More Earths than could be counted, some said. And all you had to do was walk sideways into them, one after the next, an unending chain.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17756433899145588748" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5130273967358870313">The main thing that made me interested in reading this would be the promising premise. It offered a million possibilities, and I was not disappointed. While the novel was not perfect, it was still an amazing novel that promises even more exciting things to come in the next novels in the series.<br/><br/>Embarrassingly I haven't read any of the Discworld novels of Pratchett, and none of Baxter's novels too. While anything space related has caught my interest ever since i was young, I haven't invested mysel</span>
  <span id="freeText5130273967358870313" style="display:none">The main thing that made me interested in reading this would be the promising premise. It offered a million possibilities, and I was not disappointed. While the novel was not perfect, it was still an amazing novel that promises even more exciting things to come in the next novels in the series.<br /><br />Embarrassingly I haven't read any of the Discworld novels of Pratchett, and none of Baxter's novels too. While anything space related has caught my interest ever since i was young, I haven't invested myself in reading Hard-Sci-Fi before this. Lately while browsing through goodreads though, I always get a feeling of wanting to read a good Sci-Fi book. I finally decided to pick this one up, because it's the most interesting one in my bookshelf. <br /><br />The premise didn't disappoint, and the novel has a great story. It's about the possibility of countless earths, and the repercussions of having such possibility. I really enjoy speculative fiction, or anything speculative to be concise. As a Sci-Fi fan, I have a very colorful imagination. The possibility of other earths at a reach of a step is beyond interesting. I'm glad that there are 2 more books in the series because I really want to know more of it. I know that this first novel is aimed to be an introduction to the idea of stepping, so hopefully the next ones would offer a more vivid narration of the said possibility.<br /><br />The characters are well developed and filled with humor. Joshua Valiente is the perfect main character, as he possessed wit and humor. The character of Lobsang would normally annoy me, but Pratchett managed to make me like that humor filled robot. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Sally was also a really great addition to the team. She helped Joshua develop more into a round character.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />4/5 stars. There were dull moments in the middle, but I'm still very impressed with this novel. A really satisfying first read of Pratchett and Baxter. I can't wait to read more hard sci-fi novels of the mentioned authors and also vintage/classic ones like Dick, Clarke, Reynolds, and more. Highly recommended.</span>

<div id="review_361742614" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/361742614" />
    <a title="Melissa McShane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane"><img alt="Melissa McShane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216147749p2/1329059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/361742614?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa McShane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa McShane" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane">Melissa McShane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=alien-encounters">alien-encounters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=exploration">exploration</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer361742614&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer361742614" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9223291782499177201">I <i>really</i> wanted to like this book. Turns out I liked parts of it. The idea of millions of Earths branching out from ours--a variation on different choices creating different realities--is pretty cool, as is the mechanism by which people reach them. Pratchett and Baxter gave a lot of thought to how the Earth might have developed differently under different circumstances, such as the lack of a moon altering tides and climate or even failing to deflect large asteroids from the planet. I also liked </span>
  <span id="freeText9223291782499177201" style="display:none">I <i>really</i> wanted to like this book. Turns out I liked parts of it. The idea of millions of Earths branching out from ours--a variation on different choices creating different realities--is pretty cool, as is the mechanism by which people reach them. Pratchett and Baxter gave a lot of thought to how the Earth might have developed differently under different circumstances, such as the lack of a moon altering tides and climate or even failing to deflect large asteroids from the planet. I also liked the vignettes interspersed with the main plot, about how the discovery of Stepping affected ordinary people. The characters were charming and the aliens were interesting, if not particularly original.<br /><br />Unfortunately, what should have been the high point of the story fell flat. The mystery of who or what they're going to find at the far end of the Long Earth grows increasingly suspenseful--and then the answer is...sort of blah. The reason for the disaster that's driving the aliens inward toward our Earth, the origin Earth, is in fact disastrous, but doesn't <i>feel</i> disastrous--and it's dispensed with too easily, no sacrifice (literally none, as the last sentence of the book proves) and no payoff.<br /><br />I was more dissatified with the social ramifications the authors believed would arise from this situation:<br /><br />1. Supposedly, Stepping off to new and untamed wildernesses means the economy of Datum Earth (the main world) would start to collapse. That's sort of true in the sense that if you remove enough of any vital material from an economy, it will destabilize. But the historical example of the depopulation that happened because of the Plague in the 14th century says that not only will the economy restabilize, it can do so in a way that improves the lives of the survivors. Since it's impossible to bring anything made of iron into the other Earths, the people who stay behind get to keep all the resources of civilization. And since the authors also argue that violence and crime are almost entirely caused by overpopulation, wouldn't that mean that Datum Earth would see a similar drop in crime when the poverty-stricken rise out of poverty?<br /><br />2. Except I don't actually buy the whole "crime is caused by competition for resources" thing. People kill for a lot of reasons that don't have anything to do with theft, and even if you look at one person killing another to take the second person's mate as competition for resources, it only takes three people for that to happen. The utopian settlements of the Long Earth come off, to me, as more of a social statement than a realistic possibility--a given outcome, based on assumptions that they didn't try to prove.<br /><br />Overall, it was enjoyable once, but not a book I'd want to read again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9223291782499177201" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1887755967" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1887755967" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1887755967?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1887755967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3265886806264478398"><i>“It is hard for scientists even to talk about the Long Earth without babbling about m-brane manifolds and quantum multiverses.”</i><br/><br/>It would be hard for Stephen Baxter to resist waxing lyrical about quantum whatsits I suppose. I read his <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6575201-xeelee' rel='nofollow noopener'>Xeelee</a> omnibus and only understood half of the science expositions (and that is an optimistic estimate). However, with <i>The Long Earth</i> Baxter has the late great Terry Pratchett to help make the science part more palatable and also infuse the novel with the magic of hi</span>
  <span id="freeText3265886806264478398" style="display:none"><i>“It is hard for scientists even to talk about the Long Earth without babbling about m-brane manifolds and quantum multiverses.”</i><br><br>It would be hard for Stephen Baxter to resist waxing lyrical about quantum whatsits I suppose. I read his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6575201-xeelee" rel="nofollow noopener">Xeelee</a> omnibus and only understood half of the science expositions (and that is an optimistic estimate). However, with <i>The Long Earth</i> Baxter has the late great Terry Pratchett to help make the science part more palatable and also infuse the novel with the magic of his wit, characterization, and of course, humour. I have not read the Baxter &amp; Clarke collaborations yet (coming soon), but they seem to be very like-minded gentlemen, and while I look forward to reading some mind blowing super science from their books I think the pairing with Pratchett seems like a much more dynamic combination; two very different talents that beautifully complement each other.<br><br><i>The Long Earth</i> is basically about parallel worlds, more specifically parallel Earths. This is hardly a new sci-fi conceit but this book—first in a series of five— explores more of the possibilities, ramifications, and science than other parallel universe books I have read. In <i>The Long Earth</i>, a scientist invents a simple device that enables the user to step into a parallel Earth, in the dimension adjacent to ours, also the next adjacent one, on and on presumably infinitely. <br><br><i>“Earths, untold Earths. More Earths than could be counted, some said. And all you had to do was walk sideways into them, one after the next, an unending chain.”</i><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485651388i/21836258._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>The Stepper schematic (powered by a potato!)<br><br>The design and circuitry of the “Stepper” device are shared on the internet and immediately becomes a worldwide sensation. As millions of people migrate to an alternate Earth, there is suddenly plenty of room and resources for everyone. This puts an end to scarcity, overpopulation and even poverty. The narrative is mostly focused on the adventures of Joshua Valienté and an AI entity called Lobsang across “the Long Earth” (the term for all the parallel Earths). They are later joined by a woman called Sally Linsay. Joshua and Sally are “natural steppers” who can travel without a Stepper device, as for Lobsang, there is very little he can not do. Beside this major story arc, the narrative occasionally switches to a young girl called Helen Green who has migrated to an alternate Earth to set up a colony with her family. There is also a small plot strand about a policewoman called Monica Jansson who is keeping an eye on malcontents who are unable to travel to a parallel Earth due to some innate disability. All the story threads are good, but the main story arc with Joshua and Lobsang is the most epic, as they travel further from “Datum Earth” (our Earth) than anyone else and discover all kinds of strange life forms and discover a danger that threatens every single iteration of Earth. <br><br>I remember when <i>The Long Earth</i> came out in 2012 there were many readers who expressed their disappointment with the book. That is why I am reading it now five years later. I have to say this book is a blast, very enjoyable and imaginative. I suspect many of the people who are disappointed with it are die-hard Discworld fans. <i>The Long Earth</i> is very different in tone from a typical Discworld book in that humour is not placed at the front and center of the narrative, it is not even a major component. The emphasis is here is on adventures, colonization, pioneering and exploration; basically <i>The Long Earth</i> is a romp. The wit and charm of Terry Pratchett’s prose and characterization are there in abundance, coupled with Stephen Baxter’s hard sci-fi inventiveness. I have speculated that some Terry Pratchett’s fans may be disappointed by the emphasis on adventures rather than humour, but what about Stephen Baxter’s fans? Well, there is less emphasis on science also, much less than you would find in a solo Baxter book. This is conceivably disappointing for hardcore hard sci-fi fans who probably enjoy Baxter’s normal detailed quantum physics expositions. However, for me, there is just the right amount of science to make the story plausible and fascinating. This collaboration is a synergistic success and I think Baxter is very lucky to have the opportunity to work with Pratchett for five books, and I think sci-fi fans are lucky that these two excellent gentlemen decided to combine forces. I am looking forward to reading the rest of this series. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485615290i/21833089.jpg" alt="earths" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><b>Notes:</b><br>• Video interview: <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Zif_GfGesf0" rel="nofollow noopener">Terry Pratchett and Stephen Baxter on The Long Earth</a><br>• <a href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/mar/15/sir-terry-pratchett-1948-2015-an-appreciation" rel="nofollow noopener">Sir Terry Pratchett 1948-2015: an appreciation</a> by Stephen Baxter - a wonderful article.<br><br><b>Quotes:</b><br><i>“Oh, there are certainly others smarter than you, they are stacked high in the universities achieving little or nothing. But smart has to have a depth as well as a length. Some smart brushes over a problem. And some smart grinds exceeding slow, like the mills of God, and it grinds fine, and when it comes up with an answer, it has been tested.” <br><br>‘You know I crapped my pants in the course of the engagement.’<br>‘Should I think less of you for that? Throughout the animal kingdom, it has always made good sense to jettison the cargo in a threatening situation.’<br><br>“A smiley, he thought: one curve and two dots, and you see a human face. What was the minimum you needed to see a human being? What has to be said, what has to be laughed?”<br><br>“It was once said of an English politician that if you kicked him in the butt not a muscle would move on his face until he had decided what to do about it.”</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="3265886806264478398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_627470843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/627470843" />
    <a title="Ms. Smartarse" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13703358-ms-smartarse"><img alt="Ms. Smartarse" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1401273675p2/13703358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/627470843?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms. Smartarse" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms. Smartarse" href="/user/show/13703358-ms-smartarse">Ms. Smartarse</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552164085" href="/book/show/16113693-the-long-earth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer627470843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13109207751291608604">In 2015 humanity discovers a (potato-powered) device that enables it to travel to parallel worlds. While most people need these devices to travel between worlds (i.e. <em>step</em>), there are <em>natural steppers</em> who don't rely on them, and also some that can't step at all, having to be <em>carried</em> over by others.<br/><br/>Natural stepper Joshua Valiente and Lobsang, the super computer, decide to test the limits of these parallel worlds and go on the journey of their ugh... lives. In the meantime, the rest of humanity de</span>
  <span id="freeText13109207751291608604" style="display:none">In 2015 humanity discovers a (potato-powered) device that enables it to travel to parallel worlds. While most people need these devices to travel between worlds (i.e. <em>step</em>), there are <em>natural steppers</em> who don't rely on them, and also some that can't step at all, having to be <em>carried</em> over by others.<br><br>Natural stepper Joshua Valiente and Lobsang, the super computer, decide to test the limits of these parallel worlds and go on the journey of their ugh... lives. In the meantime, the rest of humanity decides it's time to start over, and begin colonizing the newly discovered worlds, or at least that's the plan. Nature however, seems to have a few choice words about that. Oh and there's also the humans left behind due to their inability to step, who don't seem to be up to any good.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1465676982i/19388546.jpg" alt="Dexter blinds an amish man with a potato-powered light" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I frequently pick up novels authored by Sir <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett" title="Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Terry Pratchett</a>, and then often find myself utterly underwhelmed by it all. Still, I his particular brand of humor, keeps making me come back over and over again.<br><br>So imagine my surprise when I hardly found any of that in this book, yet I still ended up liking it a lot. As a matter of fact, if it weren't for the name Lobsang, I wouldn't have even recognized my favorite author's style.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1465676982i/19388547.jpg" width="400" alt="Where's the joke?" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I liked the whole science behind the concept of a "<em>Long Earth</em>". You can clearly see that someone put a whole lot of thought into it, and didn't just stop at the first random idea. For some reason, the story reminds me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/70535.2001_A_Space_Odyssey__Space_Odyssey___1_" title="2001 A Space Odyssey (Space Odyssey, #1) by Arthur C. Clarke" rel="nofollow noopener">2001 A Space Odyssey</a>: there's something about the atmosphere that made me want to continue.<br><br><strong>Score: 4/5 stars</strong><br><br>It's best to read this book without expecting <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett" title="Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Terry Pratchett</a>'s fingerprint at every corner. I don't mean that in a bad way, quite the contrary, but for those of you waiting to see the entertaining aspect from the <em>Discworld</em> novels... well, you'll be heavily disappointed. <br><br>Just give it the benefit of doubt, and there's a high chance you'll like it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13109207751291608604" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_518808380" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/518808380" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5649720-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1322357734p2/5649720.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/518808380?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5649720-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5649720-rebecca?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer518808380" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9019975461480105824">You know how famous authors will occasionally complain about how readers will come up to them at cons and tell them that they have this amazing idea for a book; the author should write the reader's idea, and then they can split the money. The moral to this kind of story is always that this is a ridiculously ignorant concept--ideas are easy, it's execution that's hard.<br/><br/>This is a novel in which two extremely prolific authors forgot this. <br/><br/>Well, to be honest, calling this a "novel" strikes me as gene</span>
  <span id="freeText9019975461480105824" style="display:none">You know how famous authors will occasionally complain about how readers will come up to them at cons and tell them that they have this amazing idea for a book; the author should write the reader's idea, and then they can split the money. The moral to this kind of story is always that this is a ridiculously ignorant concept--ideas are easy, it's execution that's hard.<br /><br />This is a novel in which two extremely prolific authors forgot this. <br /><br />Well, to be honest, calling this a "novel" strikes me as generous. There are some people in it; I've seen ten page short stories that featured more memorable characters with better developed arcs. Some things happen, but they're not really so much a plot as just some events, with some slightly bigger events kinda near the end that don't actually resolve anything. There are settings--oh, so many settings. But while each one has the potential to be a thought experiment, in actuality, each one receives a brief description and no development. Most role-playing supplements do a better job of creating intriguing worlds. More than anything else, this feels like a novel-length list of ideas for a forty book series, each of which could be developed into a story but hasn't been yet. <br /><br />The book concludes with a note that the story begins in Madison, WI, because the authors were there for a con and so could do research. I would have guessed that they looked at Google Maps for five minutes, for all the individuality the Madison setting displays. Umm, there's a government building. And some cafes. And I think there was a lake or something. <br /><br />This is a terrible thing to say, but I'm kind of hoping that Pratchett only agreed to this because he was worried about having enough money for his medical care and was hoping he could get a couple bucks more for his heirs out of this. <br /><br />I have more to rant about, in far more specificity--from the incredible lameness of the ending to the number of issues raised and then never resolved to various gaping plot holes to the sheer number of potentially interesting ideas that are somehow presented in a way that make them completely uninteresting and then dropped. Because honestly, this book pissed me off in its incredible waste of potential. But you know what? It's not actually worth anyone's time. Mine to write them, or yours to read them. And certainly it's not worth your time to read the book itself. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9019975461480105824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_353324327" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/353324327" />
    <a title="Andrea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3727861-andrea"><img alt="Andrea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442528074p2/3727861.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/353324327?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrea" href="/user/show/3727861-andrea">Andrea</a>
      </span>

          marked it as flipped-to-the-end


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13604418-the-long-earth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=british">british</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer353324327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15459201882937851236">A did not finish read.<br/><br/>I think, in its way, this could be regarded as a form of apocalyptica. A device allowing easy jumping to countless alternate worlds (conveniently free of human populations) is invented. Many people embrace this passionately, and rush off to stake their claim in a 'land rush' with no visible end game. A small percentage can't go and others don't want to, but the effect of this mass dispersal is economies collapsing, new religions, fighting among old religions. It's a book ab</span>
  <span id="freeText15459201882937851236" style="display:none">A did not finish read.<br /><br />I think, in its way, this could be regarded as a form of apocalyptica. A device allowing easy jumping to countless alternate worlds (conveniently free of human populations) is invented. Many people embrace this passionately, and rush off to stake their claim in a 'land rush' with no visible end game. A small percentage can't go and others don't want to, but the effect of this mass dispersal is economies collapsing, new religions, fighting among old religions. It's a book about global change.<br /><br />The book doesn't focus on a single person, showing us the experiences of a half-dozen or so, and headhops through a bunch more, but the primary character is Joshua, one of the first to hop, and also a natural hopper, able to travel without the device. He's an orphan, and inclined to be a major loner, and in part his progression involves finding someone he might want to travel with.<br /><br />The book didn't work for me in a number of different ways.<br /><br />The prose scales the pinnacles of beige. The plot is leaden and there's little sense of wonder, of individuals overcoming adversity, or awe or delight. But the biggest problem is Joshua, who is Super Special and Innately Superior to Everyone, so much more in connection with the natural world, so superior in his reactions to the other worlds, so much better a logical thinker, so better able to cope. People keep telling him that, so we can be sure it's not just his opinion. He is full of a combination of disdain and dislike for almost everyone he encounters, and is also a Famous Hero. When the (strangely enormous number of) children in his neighbourhood who simultaneously decided they wanted to follow some guy on the internet's suggestion they figure out how to solder some wires into a circuit and jab them into a potato, he told them that, hey, maybe you should try turning the switch the Other Direction. Genius!<br /><br />Joshua is extremely unlikeable, and few other characters distinguish themselves in any way. I liked the female police officer a bit, but 20% in I had no Cares left in me and flipped to the end to see whether this was going anywhere interesting. And the answer to that was nowhere unexpected.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15459201882937851236" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1324059007" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1324059007" />
    <a title="B Schrodinger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger"><img alt="B Schrodinger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572325701p2/5448792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1324059007?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B Schrodinger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B Schrodinger" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger">B Schrodinger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=parallel-universes">parallel-universes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=fortean">fortean</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=first-contact">first-contact</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1324059007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4410923614384493786">I did not expect that much going into this book, I was just hoping for a fun fluffy read. I borrowed it from the library, so the worst case scenario was returning it unfinished. But I was surprised and delighted by this novel and I'm jumping right into the next book.<br/><br/>In a plot that you may feel has been done to death, a mad scientist disappears and leaves instructions to build a stepping device on the internet. Kids throughout the world make a copy and end up travelling into a parallel world. It </span>
  <span id="freeText4410923614384493786" style="display:none">I did not expect that much going into this book, I was just hoping for a fun fluffy read. I borrowed it from the library, so the worst case scenario was returning it unfinished. But I was surprised and delighted by this novel and I'm jumping right into the next book.<br /><br />In a plot that you may feel has been done to death, a mad scientist disappears and leaves instructions to build a stepping device on the internet. Kids throughout the world make a copy and end up travelling into a parallel world. It seems that there are multiple Earth's all lined up and you can either travel in one direction or another, named East or West to a parallel Earth. Hasn't this been done before? Yes. Has it been done like this? My argument is no.<br /><br />Anyone who knows my taste in books knows that I appreciate the info-dump. I appreciate a great amount of detail as long as it is fascinating. And that is what you get here. I also appreciate thought experiments that explore the implications of scenarios to the best of their ability. I have hot it recently with Locke and Key and The Fifteen Lives of Henry August. This novel explores the implications of these parallel Earth's and throws in some great ideas. There is not too much of a plot at all, and I see this reflected in the bad reviews on here. This is a book that explores ideas rather than human relationships.<br /><br />I'm going to probably get some gasps at this statement: I don't really enjoy Terry Pratchett's humour. I've read a handful of Discworld novels and they are slightly entertaining, but I liken them to candy floss. Sweet, but forgettable. No sustenance. and the humour can be laid on a bit thick. Yes, Sir Terry I understood you are trying to be witty and intelligent. I was prepared for a bit of that here, but it is very toned down. There is some zaniness in parts, but it is all explained within the system, it never feels zany for the sake of zany.<br /><br />So a 4 star read for me, and probably any other nerd who loves info-dumps and big ideas. People looking for plot and character development can stick to the annual prize-winners.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4410923614384493786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2747761014" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2747761014" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2747761014?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/17564695-the-long-earth">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2747761014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16786567281379998870">This is the second time I'm reading this book but the first time I tried the audio. And I can happily report that both forms are great.<br/><br/><i>The Long Earth</i> series is not one of those action-laden scifi stories. Rather, it's more of a smart and funny thought experiment with the occasional action - making it far more realistic.<br/><br/>We are on Earth. Earth as we know it. It's called Datum Earth in this series. One day, someone publishes the plan to the so-called Stepper. A device that let's you go left or righ</span>
  <span id="freeText16786567281379998870" style="display:none">This is the second time I'm reading this book but the first time I tried the audio. And I can happily report that both forms are great.<br /><br /><i>The Long Earth</i> series is not one of those action-laden scifi stories. Rather, it's more of a smart and funny thought experiment with the occasional action - making it far more realistic.<br /><br />We are on Earth. Earth as we know it. It's called Datum Earth in this series. One day, someone publishes the plan to the so-called Stepper. A device that let's you go left or right, from this Earth to a parallel one - and back. As it turns out, it actually works and there doesn't seem to be a limit to the amounts of Earths left and right to our original one.<br />These other Earths are similar to ours but always slightly different. The common denominator is that no other Earth seems to have homo sapiens.<br />People start fleeing from the restraints of taxation and laws, stepping farther and farther out, building settlements or trying to find a quick and easy way to get rich (which isn't working because the economy they know is collapsing, naturally).<br />Enter Joshua. A boy born on one of the Long Earths by accident. He's one of a not yet disclosed number of "natural steppers", meaning he doesn't actually need the device and knew about stepping before the Long Earth was opened up to (almost) everybody.<br />There is also Officer Monica Jansson who tries to hold everything together now that so many are leaving and governments are too dumbfounded and slow to react.<br />Moreover, there is an entity called Lobsang - formerly a Tibetan mechanic first trapped in a beverage vending machine that later becomes co-owner of a big company. He has copied himself into many systems and loves to learn. thus has the financial means as well as leverage to research the Long Earth together with Joshua.<br />And then there are the people who can't step at all (might be genetic) as well as all the other disgruntled idiots that try to ruin this great and potentially species-saving opportunity.<br /><br />Other things we get are:<br />- a glimpse at a settlement that is built about 100.000 Earths away from Datum Earth<br />- strange other life forms that are designated as elves and trolls<br />- different variations on our continents and oceans incl. slightly different fauna and flora<br />- the mysterious Happy Landing<br />- the Migraine Monster<br /><br />This is a true pioneer / frontier adventure. It's about exploration. The exploration of the human spirit, of loneliness and a sense of community in various forms. The exploration of a sheer limitless number of planets and what their existence (as well as free access to them for everyone) means to politics, religion and economics.<br /><br />I loved this as much as the first time around (though hearing the narrator sing the songs to the trolls gave this version a certain bonus). A great scifi trip that gets spiced up by Terry Pratchett's signature sense of humour and sharp social observations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16786567281379998870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Juan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5685009-juan"><img alt="Juan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/403711682?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juan" href="/user/show/5685009-juan">Juan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5685009-juan?shelf=scif-fi">scif-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer403711682" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5996286129546415888">I finished The Long Earth yesterday, and came to see what others thought, and in many respects I agree; The Long Earth has an incredibly novel premise, but a storyline that in the end, doesn't really go anywhere (no pun intended).<br/><br/>The book essentially offers a thought exercise of what would happen if relatively unlimited inter-planetary (or more correctly, inter-dimensional) travel were available to the masses on Earth. What would people do? Would there be a mass exodus? Would people try to explo</span>
  <span id="freeText5996286129546415888" style="display:none">I finished The Long Earth yesterday, and came to see what others thought, and in many respects I agree; The Long Earth has an incredibly novel premise, but a storyline that in the end, doesn't really go anywhere (no pun intended).<br /><br />The book essentially offers a thought exercise of what would happen if relatively unlimited inter-planetary (or more correctly, inter-dimensional) travel were available to the masses on Earth. What would people do? Would there be a mass exodus? Would people try to exploit this travel for their own gain or exploiting others? How would it affect the economy of Earth and the people who remained? What would remain valuable and what wouldn't? Would governments remain intact? So many questions and fascinating to think about.<br /><br />Unfortunately, the storyline that is the vehicle for this premise is not quite as strong. I won't go into it much as it would spoil things, but I felt rather unfulfilled at the end. I was sure that this coming terror would have been much more metaphysical and not so...meh.<br /><br />All in all, I would still highly recommend this just to change the way you think, if only a little. =)<br /><br />oh...and I really want a t-shirt that says "Don't worry! On another Earth it already happened" like Joshua.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5996286129546415888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_267142716" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/267142716" />
    <a title="Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1605591-christine"><img alt="Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252166169p2/1605591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/267142716?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christine" href="/user/show/1605591-christine">Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780857520098" href="/book/show/13003102-the-long-earth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=sir-terry-pratchett">sir-terry-pratchett</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer267142716" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12875661426460865081">Those poor French. You lose to Spain at the Euro and Terry Pratchett takes an accurate pot shot at you in this book.<br/><br/>C'est la vie.<br/><br/>I'm unsure to give this three or four stars. I really am. I didn't quite really like it, but I didn't just like it. I went with four because it is my default when I read such a book and it makes me think.<br/><br/>The idea behind this book will be slightly familiar to those who watched Sliders or who have read comic books. The twist is that most people can step to the words eith</span>
  <span id="freeText12875661426460865081" style="display:none">Those poor French. You lose to Spain at the Euro and Terry Pratchett takes an accurate pot shot at you in this book.<br /><br />C'est la vie.<br /><br />I'm unsure to give this three or four stars. I really am. I didn't quite really like it, but I didn't just like it. I went with four because it is my default when I read such a book and it makes me think.<br /><br />The idea behind this book will be slightly familiar to those who watched Sliders or who have read comic books. The twist is that most people can step to the words either by natural talent or with a device that is readily availble. There is a small minority of humanity that can't do this.<br /><br />The thing is, unlike Sliders or comic book, Baxter and Pratchett really look at what this would mean to politics, family, and economy. This is the best writing of the book (and give rise to the shot at the French). It is this ascept that really gets your to think. <br /><br />The down side is that there is another plot involing Joshua and his employer (who really is from Discworld) who are travelling the multiple earths. In some ways, this feels like a quest in search of a plot. And while it allows for wonderful Geekdom references that any Sci-Fi fan will love, I actually wanted to follow the minor characters more because they were most intersting.<br /><br />Though I loved the cat digression.<br /><br />The book jacket hints that this will be the first in the series (the ending is slightly cliffhangerish. It can go either way). If that is true, this book than seems more of a door opening world building to allow the two gentleman too play more (which would be great).<br /><br />The writing styles do seem to merge, there are flashes of Pratchett wit, though no footnotes - but this is a collarbortion not a straight Pratchett. But I enjoyed Baxter's Mammoth series, and after this book, I think I'll try some more by Baxter.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12875661426460865081" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
